<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Smallpox</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Eradicated viral disease</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Variola" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Variola_(disambiguation)" title="Variola (disambiguation)">Variola (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup> red plague<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peg2017_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peg2017-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" The bumps filled with thick fluid and a depression or dimple in the center are characteristic.</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medical_specialty" title="Medical specialty">Specialty</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease_(medical_specialty)" title="Infectious disease (medical specialty)">Infectious disease</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Signs_and_symptoms" title="Signs and symptoms">Symptoms</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><b>Early</b>: Fever, vomiting, mouth sores<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Later</b>: Fluid filled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blisters" title="Blisters">blisters</a> which <a href="/wiki/Wound_healing#Proliferative_phase" title="Wound healing">scab</a> over<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication (medicine)">Complications</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Scarring of the skin, blindness<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016What_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016What-6">[6]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Usual onset</th><td class="infobox-data">1 to 3 weeks following exposure<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Duration</th><td class="infobox-data">About 4 weeks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Causes</th><td class="infobox-data"><i>Variola major</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alastrim" title="Alastrim">Variola minor</a></i> (spread between people)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016What_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016What-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-She2004_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-She2004-7">[7]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">Diagnostic method</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Based on symptoms and confirmed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PCR" title="PCR">PCR</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2017Diag_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2017Diag-8">[8]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Differential_diagnosis" title="Differential diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">Chickenpox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Impetigo" title="Impetigo">impetigo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molluscum_contagiosum" title="Molluscum contagiosum">molluscum contagiosum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monkeypox" title="Monkeypox">monkeypox</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2017Diag_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2017Diag-8">[8]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Prevention</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Smallpox_vaccine" title="Smallpox vaccine">Smallpox vaccine</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2017Tx_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2017Tx-9">[9]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Treatment</th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supportive_care" title="Supportive care">Supportive care</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">Medication</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Brincidofovir" title="Brincidofovir">Brincidofovir</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Prognosis" title="Prognosis">Prognosis</a></th><td class="infobox-data">30% risk of death<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">Eradicated</a> (last wild case in 1977)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Smallpox</b> was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a> caused by one of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virus_(biology)" title="Virus (biology)">virus</a> variants, <i><a href="#Cause">Variola major</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Variola_minor" title="Variola minor">Variola minor</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-She2004_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-She2004-7">[7]</a></sup> The agent of variola virus (VARV) belongs to the genus <a href="/wiki/Orthopoxvirus" title="Orthopoxvirus">Orthopoxvirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viruses2015_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Viruses2015-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ali_Maow_Maalin#Maalin's_case" title="Ali Maow Maalin">last naturally occurring case</a> was diagnosed in October 1977, and the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) certified the <a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">global eradication</a> of the disease in 1980,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup> making it the only human disease to be eradicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The initial symptoms of the disease included <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a> and vomiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup> This was followed by formation of <a href="/wiki/Ulcer_(dermatology)" title="Ulcer (dermatology)">ulcers</a> in the mouth and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_rash" title="Skin rash">skin rash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup> Over a number of days, the skin rash turned into the characteristic fluid-filled <a href="/wiki/Blister" title="Blister">blisters</a> with a dent in the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup> The bumps then <a href="/wiki/Wound_healing#Proliferative_phase" title="Wound healing">scabbed</a> over and fell off, leaving scars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Sym_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Sym-5">[5]</a></sup> The disease was spread between people or via <a href="/wiki/Fomite" title="Fomite">contaminated objects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016What_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016What-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leb2013_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leb2013-13">[13]</a></sup> Prevention was achieved mainly through the <a href="/wiki/Smallpox_vaccine" title="Smallpox vaccine">smallpox vaccine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2017Tx_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2017Tx-9">[9]</a></sup> Once the disease had developed, certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiviral_medication" title="Antiviral medication">antiviral medication</a> may have helped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2017Tx_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2017Tx-9">[9]</a></sup> The risk of death was about 30%, with higher rates among babies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016What_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016What-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rie2005_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rie2005-14">[14]</a></sup> Often, those who survived had extensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scarring" title="Scarring">scarring</a> of their skin, and some were left blind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016What_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016What-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest evidence of the disease dates to around 1500 BCE in Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Mummy" title="Mummy">mummies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2017His_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2017His-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The disease historically occurred in <a href="/wiki/Disease_outbreak" title="Disease outbreak">outbreaks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup> In 18th-century Europe, it is estimated that 400,000 people died from the disease per year, and that one-third of all cases of blindness were due to smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hays2005_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hays2005-17">[17]</a></sup> Smallpox is estimated to have killed up to 300 million people in the 20th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and around 500 million people in the last 100 years of its existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Earlier deaths included six European <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarchs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hays2005_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hays2005-17">[17]</a></sup> As recently as 1967, 15 million cases occurred a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Inoculation" title="Inoculation">Inoculation</a> for smallpox appears to have started in China around the 1500s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Europe adopted this practice from Asia in the first half of the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strathern179_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-strathern179-23">[23]</a></sup> In 1796, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a> introduced the modern smallpox vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolfe2002_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolfe2002-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In 1967, the WHO intensified efforts to eliminate the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup> Smallpox is one of two infectious diseases to have been eradicated, the other being <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">rinderpest</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The term "smallpox" was first used in Britain in the early 16th century to distinguish the disease from <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, which was then known as the "great pox".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bar1997_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bar1997-29">[29]</a></sup> Other historical names for the disease include pox, speckled monster, and red plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peg2017_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peg2017-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bar1997_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bar1997-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable sortable" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; border: none;">

<p>There are two forms of the smallpox virus. <i>Variola major</i> is the severe and most common form, with a more extensive rash and higher fever. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alastrim" title="Alastrim">Variola minor</a></i> is a less common presentation, causing less severe disease, typically discrete smallpox, with historical death rates of 1% or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FactSheet_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FactSheet-31">[31]</a></sup> Subclinical (<a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic" title="Asymptomatic">asymptomatic</a>) infections with <i>Variola virus</i> were noted but were not common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> In addition, a form called <i>variola sine eruptione</i> (smallpox without rash) was seen generally in vaccinated persons. This form was marked by a fever that occurred after the usual <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">incubation period</a> and could be confirmed only by <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibody</a> studies or, rarely, by <a href="/wiki/Viral_culture" title="Viral culture">viral culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> In addition, there were two very rare and fulminating types of smallpox, the malignant (flat) and hemorrhagic forms, which were usually fatal.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<p>The initial symptoms were similar to other viral diseases that are still extant, such as <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a>: fever of at least 38.3 °C (101 °F), <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle pain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">malaise</a>, headache and fatigue. As the <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">digestive tract</a> was commonly involved, nausea, vomiting, and backache often occurred. The early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prodromal" title="Prodromal">prodromal</a> stage usually lasted 2–4 days. By days 12–15, the first visible lesions – small reddish spots called <i><a href="/wiki/Enanthem" title="Enanthem">enanthem</a></i> – appeared on mucous membranes of the mouth, tongue, <a href="/wiki/Palate" title="Palate">palate</a>, and throat, and the temperature fell to near-normal. These lesions rapidly enlarged and ruptured, releasing large amounts of virus into the saliva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIP_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFIP-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smallpox virus tended to attack skin cells, causing the characteristic pimples, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macule" title="Macule">macules</a>, associated with the disease. A rash developed on the skin 24 to 48 hours after lesions on the mucous membranes appeared. Typically the macules first appeared on the forehead, then rapidly spread to the whole face, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proximal" title="Proximal">proximal</a> portions of extremities, the trunk, and lastly to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distal" title="Distal">distal</a> portions of extremities. The process took no more than 24 to 36 hours, after which no new lesions appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIP_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-AFIP-33">[33]</a></sup> At this point, <i>variola major</i> infection could take several very different courses, which resulted in four types of smallpox disease based on the Rao classification:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[34]</a></sup> ordinary, modified, malignant (or flat), and hemorrhagic smallpox. Historically, ordinary smallpox had an overall <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatality_rate" title="Fatality rate">fatality rate</a> of about 30%, and the malignant and hemorrhagic forms were usually fatal. The modified form was almost never fatal. In early hemorrhagic cases, hemorrhages occurred before any skin lesions developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eMedicine_Smallpox_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-eMedicine_Smallpox-35">[35]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">incubation period</a> between contraction and the first obvious symptoms of the disease was around 12 days.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordinary">Ordinary</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_child.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1919" data-file-width="1305" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Smallpox_child.jpg/170px-Smallpox_child.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Smallpox_child.jpg/255px-Smallpox_child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Smallpox_child.jpg/340px-Smallpox_child.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_child.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A child showing rash due to ordinary-type smallpox (<i>Variola major</i>)</div></div></div>
<p>Ninety percent or more of smallpox cases among unvaccinated persons were of the ordinary type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> In this form of the disease, by the second day of the rash the macules had become raised <i><a href="/wiki/Papule" title="Papule">papules</a></i>. By the third or fourth day, the papules had filled with an opalescent fluid to become <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesicle_(dermatology)" title="Vesicle (dermatology)">vesicles</a></i>. This fluid became <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opaque</a> and turbid within 24–48 hours, resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pustule" title="Pustule">pustules</a>.
</p><p>By the sixth or seventh day, all the skin lesions had become pustules. Between seven and ten days the pustules had matured and reached their maximum size. The pustules were sharply raised, typically round, tense, and firm to the touch. The pustules were deeply embedded in the dermis, giving them the feel of a small bead in the skin. Fluid slowly leaked from the pustules, and by the end of the second week, the pustules had deflated and began to dry up, forming crusts or scabs. By day 16–20 scabs had formed over all of the lesions, which had started to flake off, leaving <a href="/wiki/Depigmentation" title="Depigmentation">depigmented</a> scars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-training1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-training1-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ordinary smallpox generally produced a discrete rash, in which the pustules stood out on the skin separately. The distribution of the rash was most dense on the face, denser on the extremities than on the trunk, and denser on the distal parts of the extremities than on the proximal. The palms of the hands and soles of the feet were involved in most cases. Sometimes, the blisters merged into sheets, forming a confluent rash, which began to detach the outer layers of skin from the underlying flesh. Patients with confluent smallpox often remained ill even after scabs had formed over all the lesions. In one case series, the case-fatality rate in confluent smallpox was 62 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modified">Modified</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modified_smallpox2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1204" data-file-width="1637" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Modified_smallpox2.png/220px-Modified_smallpox2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Modified_smallpox2.png/330px-Modified_smallpox2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Modified_smallpox2.png/440px-Modified_smallpox2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modified_smallpox2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modified smallpox in a vaccinated 4 year old in <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, 1962</div></div></div>
<p>Referring to the character of the eruption and the rapidity of its development, modified smallpox occurred mostly in previously vaccinated people. It was rare in unvaccinated people, with one case study showing 1–2% of modified cases compared to around 25% in vaccinated people. In this form, the prodromal illness still occurred but may have been less severe than in the ordinary type. There was usually no fever during the evolution of the rash. The skin lesions tended to be fewer and evolved more quickly, were more superficial, and may not have shown the uniform characteristic of more typical smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-training1_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-training1-36">[36]</a></sup> Modified smallpox was rarely, if ever, fatal. This form of <i>variola major</i> was more easily confused with <a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">chickenpox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malignant">Malignant</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malignant_smallpox_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1498" data-file-width="1647" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Malignant_smallpox_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Malignant_smallpox_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Malignant_smallpox_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Malignant_smallpox_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Malignant_smallpox_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Malignant_smallpox_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malignant_smallpox_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Malignant hemorrhagic smallpox in a baker during an 1896 epidemic in <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. Died 8 days after admission.</div></div></div>
<p>In malignant-type smallpox (also called flat smallpox) the lesions remained almost flush with the skin at the time when raised vesicles would have formed in the ordinary type. It is unknown why some people developed this type. Historically, it accounted for 5–10 percent of cases, and most (72 percent) were children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup> Malignant smallpox was accompanied by a severe <a href="/wiki/Prodrome" title="Prodrome">prodromal</a> phase that lasted 3–4 days, prolonged high fever, and severe symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Viremia" title="Viremia">viremia</a>. The prodromal symptoms continued even after the onset of the rash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup> The rash on the mucous membranes (<a href="/wiki/Enanthem" title="Enanthem">enanthem</a>) was extensive. Skin lesions matured slowly, were typically confluent or semi-confluent, and by the seventh or eighth day, they were flat and appeared to be buried in the skin. Unlike ordinary-type smallpox, the vesicles contained little fluid, were soft and velvety to the touch, and may have contained hemorrhages. Malignant smallpox was nearly always fatal and death usually occurred between the 8th and 12th day of illness. Often, a day or two before death, the lesions turned ashen gray, which, along with abdominal distension, was a bad prognostic sign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup> This form is thought to be caused by deficient <a href="/wiki/Cell-mediated_immunity" title="Cell-mediated immunity">cell-mediated immunity</a> to smallpox. If the person recovered, the lesions gradually faded and did not form scars or scabs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hemorrhagic">Hemorrhagic</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Viral_hemorrhagic_fever" title="Viral hemorrhagic fever">Hemorrhagic</a> smallpox is a severe form accompanied by extensive bleeding into the skin, mucous membranes, gastrointestinal tract, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">viscera</a>. This form develops in approximately two percent of infections and occurs mostly in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> Pustules do not typically form in hemorrhagic smallpox. Instead, bleeding occurs under the skin, making it look charred and black,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> hence this form of the disease is also referred to as <i>variola nigra</i> or "black pox".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PrestonNY_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-PrestonNY-38">[38]</a></sup> Hemorrhagic smallpox has very rarely been caused by <i>Variola minor</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Downie_619–629_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Downie_619–629-39">[39]</a></sup> While bleeding may occur in mild cases and not affect outcomes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dixon_1962_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon_1962-40">[40]</a></sup> hemorrhagic smallpox is typically fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2011_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2011-41">[41]</a></sup> Vaccination does not appear to provide any immunity to either form of hemorrhagic smallpox and some cases even occurred among people that were revaccinated shortly before. It has two forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early">Early</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hemorrhagic_smallpox_2_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="362" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Hemorrhagic_smallpox_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Hemorrhagic_smallpox_2_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Hemorrhagic_smallpox_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Hemorrhagic_smallpox_2_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/Hemorrhagic_smallpox_2_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hemorrhagic_smallpox_2_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Likely hemorrhagic smallpox during a 1925 <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> epidemic in a patient who later died. Patient described as an unvaccinated <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Scientist</a>, who "thought that he could by power of mind prevent smallpox."</div></div></div>
<p>The early or <a href="/wiki/Fulminant" title="Fulminant">fulminant</a> form of hemorrhagic smallpox (referred to as <i>purpura variolosa</i>) begins with a prodromal phase characterized by a high fever, severe headache, and abdominal pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The skin becomes dusky and erythematous, and this is rapidly followed by the development of <a href="/wiki/Petechia" title="Petechia">petechiae</a> and bleeding in the skin, <a href="/wiki/Subconjunctival_bleeding" title="Subconjunctival bleeding">conjunctiva</a> and mucous membranes. Death often occurs suddenly between the fifth and seventh days of illness, when only a few insignificant skin lesions are present. Some people survive a few days longer, during which time the skin detaches and fluid accumulates under it, rupturing at the slightest injury. People are usually conscious until death or shortly before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2011_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2011-41">[41]</a></sup> Autopsy reveals <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petechiae" title="Petechiae">petechiae</a> and bleeding in the spleen, kidney, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serous_membranes" title="Serous membranes">serous membranes</a>, skeletal muscles, <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardium</a>, liver, gonads and bladder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Downie_619–629_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Downie_619–629-39">[39]</a></sup> Historically, this condition was frequently misdiagnosed, with the correct diagnosis made only at autopsy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Downie_619–629_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Downie_619–629-39">[39]</a></sup> This form is more likely to occur in pregnant women than in the general population (approximately 16% of cases in unvaccinated pregnant women were early hemorrhagic smallpox, versus roughly 1% in nonpregnant women and adult males).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2011_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2011-41">[41]</a></sup> The case fatality rate of early hemorrhagic smallpox approaches 100%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2011_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2011-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Late">Late</span></h4>
<p>There is also a later form of hemorrhagic smallpox (referred to late hemorrhagic smallpox, or <i>variolosa pustula hemorrhagica</i>). The prodrome is severe and similar to that observed in early hemorrhagic smallpox, and the fever persists throughout the course of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup> Bleeding appears in the early eruptive period (but later than that seen in <i>purpura variolosa</i>), and the rash is often flat and does not progress beyond the vesicular stage. Hemorrhages in the mucous membranes appear to occur less often than in the early hemorrhagic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> Sometimes the rash forms pustules which bleed at the base and then undergo the same process as in ordinary smallpox. This form of the disease is characterized by a decrease in all of the elements of the <a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">coagulation</a> cascade and an increase in circulating <a href="/wiki/Antithrombin" title="Antithrombin">antithrombin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIP_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-AFIP-33">[33]</a></sup> This form of smallpox occurs anywhere from three to 25 percent of fatal cases, depending on the virulence of the smallpox strain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eMedicine_Smallpox_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-eMedicine_Smallpox-35">[35]</a></sup> Most people with the late-stage form die within eight to 10 days of illness. Among the few who recover, the hemorrhagic lesions gradually disappear after a long period of convalescence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup> The case fatality rate for late hemorrhagic smallpox is around 90-95%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[34]</a></sup> Pregnant women are slightly more likely to experience this form of the disease, though not as much as early hemorrhagic smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner_1988_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner_1988-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span></h2>

<p>Smallpox was caused by infection with <i>Variola virus</i>, which belongs to the family <i><a href="/wiki/Poxviridae" title="Poxviridae">Poxviridae</a></i>, subfamily <i><a href="/wiki/Chordopoxvirinae" title="Chordopoxvirinae">Chordopoxvirinae</a></i>, and genus <i><a href="/wiki/Orthopoxvirus" title="Orthopoxvirus">Orthopoxvirus</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<p>The date of the appearance of smallpox is not settled. It most probably evolved from a terrestrial African rodent virus between 68,000 and 16,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito2006_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito2006-43">[43]</a></sup> The wide range of dates is due to the different records used to calibrate the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clock</a>. One <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> was the <i>Variola major</i> strains (the more clinically severe form of smallpox) which spread from Asia between 400 and 1,600 years ago. A second clade included both <i>Alastrim minor</i> (a phenotypically mild smallpox) described from the American continents and isolates from West Africa which diverged from an ancestral strain between 1,400 and 6,300 years before present. This clade further diverged into two subclades at least 800 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2007_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li2007-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>A second estimate has placed the separation of variola from <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Taterapox&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Taterapox (page does not exist)">Taterapox</a> (an <a href="/wiki/Orthopoxvirus" title="Orthopoxvirus">Orthopoxvirus</a> of some African rodents including <a href="/wiki/Gerbillinae" title="Gerbillinae">gerbils</a>) at 3,000 to 4,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes2010_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes2010-45">[45]</a></sup> This is consistent with archaeological and historical evidence regarding the appearance of smallpox as a human disease which suggests a relatively recent origin. If the mutation rate is assumed to be similar to that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpesvirus" title="Herpesvirus">herpesviruses</a>, the divergence date of variola from Taterapox has been estimated to be 50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes2010_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes2010-45">[45]</a></sup> While this is consistent with the other published estimates, it suggests that the archaeological and historical evidence is very incomplete. Better estimates of mutation rates in these viruses are needed.
</p><p>Examination of a strain that dates from c. 1650 found that this strain was <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> to the other presently sequenced strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duggan2016_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duggan2016-46">[46]</a></sup> The mutation rate of this virus is well modeled by a molecular clock. Diversification of strains only occurred in the 18th and 19th centuries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virology">Virology</span></h3>
<p>Variola is a large brick-shaped virus measuring approximately 302 to 350 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanometer" title="Nanometer">nanometers</a> by 244 to 270 nm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> with a single linear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DsDNA" title="DsDNA">double stranded DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> 186 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilobase_pair" title="Kilobase pair">kilobase pairs</a> (kbp) in size and containing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hairpin_loop" title="Hairpin loop">hairpin loop</a> at each end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moss_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moss-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Damon_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Damon-49">[49]</a></sup> The two classic varieties of smallpox are <i>variola major</i> and <i>variola minor</i>.
</p><p>Four orthopoxviruses cause infection in humans: variola, <a href="/wiki/Vaccinia" title="Vaccinia">vaccinia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cowpox" title="Cowpox">cowpox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Monkeypox" title="Monkeypox">monkeypox</a>. Variola infects only humans in nature, although primates and other animals have been infected in an experimental setting. Vaccinia, cowpox, and monkeypox viruses can infect both humans and other animals in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The life cycle of poxviruses is complicated by having multiple infectious forms, with differing mechanisms of cell entry. Poxviruses are unique among human DNA viruses in that they replicate in the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> of the cell rather than in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>. To replicate, poxviruses produce a variety of specialized proteins not produced by other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_viruses" title="DNA viruses">DNA viruses</a>, the most important of which is a viral-associated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase" title="DNA-dependent RNA polymerase">DNA-dependent RNA polymerase</a>.
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Viral_envelope" title="Viral envelope">enveloped</a> and unenveloped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virions" title="Virions">virions</a> are infectious. The viral envelope is made of modified <a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi</a> membranes containing viral-specific polypeptides, including <a href="/wiki/Hemagglutinin" title="Hemagglutinin">hemagglutinin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moss_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moss-48">[48]</a></sup> Infection with either <i>variola major</i> or <i>variola minor</i> confers immunity against the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIP_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-AFIP-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Variola_Major"><i>Variola Major</i></span></h4>
<p>The more common, infectious form of the virus comes from the <i>Variola major</i> strain.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Variola_minor"><i>Variola minor</i></span></h4>
<p><i>Variola minor</i>, also called alastrim, was a less common form of the virus, and much less deadly. Although <i>Variola minor</i> had the same incubation period and pathogenetic stages as smallpox, it is believed to have had a mortality rate of less than 1%, as compared to smallpox's 30%. Like <i>Variola major</i>, <i>Variola minor</i> was spread through inhalation of the virus in the air, which could occur through face-to-face contact or through fomites. Infection with <i>Variola minor</i> conferred immunity against the more dangerous variola major.
</p><p>Because variola minor was a less debilitating disease than smallpox, patients were more frequently ambulant and thus able to infect others more rapidly. As such, <i>Variola minor</i> swept through the United States, Great Britain, and South Africa in the early 20th century, becoming the dominant form of the disease in those areas and thus rapidly decreasing mortality rates. Along with Variola major, the minor form has now been totally eradicated from the globe. The last case of indigenous <i>Variola minor</i> was reported in a Somali cook, Ali Maow Maalin, in October 1977, and smallpox was officially declared eradicated worldwide in May 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <i>Variola minor</i> was also called white pox, kaffir pox, Cuban itch, West Indian pox, milk pox, and pseudovariola.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genome_composition">Genome composition</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of <i>Variola major</i> is about 186,000 base pairs in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> It is made from linear double stranded <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and contains the <a href="/wiki/Coding_region" title="Coding region">coding sequence</a> for about 200 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup> The genes are usually not overlapping and typically occur in blocks that point towards the closer terminal region of the genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> The coding sequence of the central region of the genome is highly consistent across <a href="/wiki/Orthopoxvirus" title="Orthopoxvirus">orthopoxviruses</a>, and the arrangement of genes is consistent across chordopoxviruses<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The center of the variola genome contains the majority of the essential viral genes, including the genes for structural <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(biology)" title="Transcription (biology)">transcription</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">mRNA</a> synthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup> The ends of the genome vary more across strains and species of <a href="/wiki/Orthopoxvirus" title="Orthopoxvirus">orthopoxviruses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup> These regions contain proteins that <a href="/wiki/Immunomodulation" title="Immunomodulation">modulate</a> the hosts' immune systems, and are primarily responsible for the variability in <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Orthopoxvirus" title="Orthopoxvirus">orthopoxvirus</a> family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup> These terminal regions in poxviruses are <a href="/wiki/Inverted_repeat" title="Inverted repeat">inverted terminal repetitions</a> (ITR) sequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> These sequences are identical but oppositely oriented on either end of the genome, leading to the genome being a continuous loop of DNA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> Components of the ITR sequences include an incompletely base paired A/T rich <a href="/wiki/Stem-loop" title="Stem-loop">hairpin loop</a>, a region of roughly 100 base pairs necessary for resolving <a href="/wiki/Concatemer" title="Concatemer">concatomeric</a> DNA (a stretch of DNA containing multiple copies of the same sequence), a few <a href="/wiki/Open_reading_frame" title="Open reading frame">open reading frames</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Microsatellite" title="Microsatellite">short tandemly repeating sequences</a> of varying number and length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> The ITRs of <a href="/wiki/Poxviridae" title="Poxviridae">poxviridae</a> vary in length across strains and species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-5"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> The coding sequence for most of the viral proteins in <i>Variola major</i> have at least 90% similarity with the genome of <i><a href="/wiki/Vaccinia" title="Vaccinia">Vaccinia</a></i>, a related virus used for <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> against smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-6"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gene_expression">Gene expression</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">Gene expression</a> of variola occurs entirely within the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> of the host <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a>, and follows a distinct progression during infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-7"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> After entry of an infectious <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virion</a> into a host cell, synthesis of viral <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">mRNA</a> can be detected within 20 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-8"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> About half of the viral genome is <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(biology)" title="Transcription (biology)">transcribed</a> prior to the <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">replication</a> of viral DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-9"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> The first set of expressed genes are transcribed by pre-existing viral machinery packaged within the infecting virion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-10"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> These genes encode the factors necessary for viral DNA synthesis and for transcription of the next set of expressed genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-11"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> Unlike most DNA viruses, <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a> in variola and other poxviruses takes place within the cytoplasm of the infected cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-12"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> The exact timing of DNA replication after infection of a host cell varies across the <a href="/wiki/Poxviridae" title="Poxviridae">poxviridae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-13"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">Recombination</a> of the genome occurs within actively infected cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-14"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> Following the onset of viral DNA replication, an intermediate set of genes codes for <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> of late gene expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-15"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gene_product" title="Gene product">products</a> of the later genes include transcription factors necessary for transcribing the early genes for new virions, as well as viral <a href="/wiki/RNA_polymerase" title="RNA polymerase">RNA polymerase</a> and other essential enzymes for new viral particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-16"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> These proteins are then packaged into new infectious virions capable of infecting other cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-17"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h3>
<p>Two live samples of <i>Variola major</i> remain, one in the United States at the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a> in Atlanta, and one at the <a href="/wiki/State_Research_Center_of_Virology_and_Biotechnology_VECTOR" title="State Research Center of Virology and Biotechnology VECTOR">Vector Institute</a> in Koltsovo, Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54">[54]</a></sup> Research with the remaining virus samples is tightly controlled, and each research proposal must be approved by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Assembly" title="World Health Assembly">World Health Assembly</a> (WHA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54">[54]</a></sup> Most research on poxviruses is performed using the closely related <i><a href="/wiki/Vaccinia" title="Vaccinia">Vaccinia virus</a></i> as a model organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-18"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> Vaccinia virus, which is used to vaccinate for smallpox, is also under research as a <a href="/wiki/Viral_vector_vaccine" title="Viral vector vaccine">viral vector</a> for <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> for unrelated diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The genome of <i>Variola major</i> was first <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">sequenced</a> in its entirety in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup> The complete coding sequence is publicly available online.The current <a href="/wiki/Reference_genome" title="Reference genome">reference sequence</a> for <i>Variola major</i> was sequenced from a strain that circulated in India in 1967. In addition, there are sequences for samples of other strains that were collected during the WHO eradication campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Genome_browser" title="Genome browser">genome browser</a> for a complete database of annotated sequences of <i>Variola</i> and other poxviruses is publicly available through the <a href="/wiki/Viral_Bioinformatics_Resource_Center" title="Viral Bioinformatics Resource Center">Viral Bioinformatics Resource Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_engineering">Genetic engineering</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> currently bans <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> of the smallpox virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-57">[57]</a></sup> However, in 2004, a committee advisory to the WHO voted in favor of allowing editing of the genome of the two remaining samples of Variola major to add a <a href="/wiki/Marker_gene" title="Marker gene">marker gene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-57">[57]</a></sup> This gene, called <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">GFP</a>, or green fluorescent protein, would cause live samples of the virus to glow green under fluorescent light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-58">[58]</a></sup> The insertion of this gene, which would not influence the <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a> of the virus, would be the only allowed modification of the genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-58">[58]</a></sup> The committee stated the proposed modification would aid in research of treatments by making it easier to assess whether a potential treatment was effective in killing viral samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-58">[58]</a></sup> The recommendation could only take effect if approved by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Assembly" title="World Health Assembly">WHA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-:5-58">[58]</a></sup> When the WHA discussed the proposal in 2005, it refrained from taking a formal vote on the proposal, stating that it would review individual research proposals one at a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Addition of the GFP gene to the <i><a href="/wiki/Vaccinia" title="Vaccinia">Vaccinia</a></i> genome is routinely performed during research on the closely related <i><a href="/wiki/Vaccinia" title="Vaccinia">Vaccinia virus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span></h4>
<p>The public availability of variola's complete sequence has raised concerns about the possibility of illicit synthesis of infectious virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-61">[61]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Vaccinia" title="Vaccinia">Vaccinia</a></i>, a cousin of the smallpox virus, was artificially synthesized in 2002 by <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">NIH</a> scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-62">[62]</a></sup> They used a previously established method that involved using a <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant</a> viral genome to create a self-replicating bacterial <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> that produced viral particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, another group synthesized the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsepox" title="Horsepox">horsepox</a> virus using publicly available sequence data for horsepox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-63">[63]</a></sup> The researchers argued that their work would be beneficial to creating a safer and more effective <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccine</a> for smallpox, although an effective vaccine is already available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-63">[63]</a></sup> The horsepox virus had previously seemed to have gone extinct, raising concern about potential revival of <i>Variola major</i> and causing other scientists to question their motives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-61">[61]</a></sup> Critics found it especially concerning that the group was able to recreate viable virus in a short time frame with relatively little cost or effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-:8-63">[63]</a></sup> Although the WHO bans individual laboratories from <a href="/wiki/Artificial_gene_synthesis" title="Artificial gene synthesis">synthesizing</a> more than 20% of the genome at a time, and purchases of smallpox genome fragments are monitored and regulated, a group with <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">malicious intentions</a> could compile, from multiple sources, the full synthetic genome necessary to produce viable virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-:8-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>Transmission occurred through inhalation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airborne_disease" title="Airborne disease">airborne</a> <i>Variola virus</i>, usually droplets expressed from the oral, nasal, or <a href="/wiki/Pharynx" title="Pharynx">pharyngeal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mucosa" title="Mucosa">mucosa</a> of an infected person. It was transmitted from one person to another primarily through prolonged face-to-face contact with an infected person, usually, within a distance of 1.8 m (6 feet), but could also be spread through direct contact with infected bodily fluids or contaminated objects (<a href="/wiki/Fomite" title="Fomite">fomites</a>) such as bedding or clothing. Rarely, smallpox was spread by virus carried in the air in enclosed settings such as buildings, buses, and trains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FactSheet_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FactSheet-31">[31]</a></sup> The virus can cross the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>, but the incidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital" title="Congenital">congenital</a> smallpox was relatively low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIP_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-AFIP-33">[33]</a></sup> Smallpox was not notably infectious in the <a href="/wiki/Prodrome" title="Prodrome">prodromal</a> period and viral shedding was usually delayed until the appearance of the rash, which was often accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Lesion" title="Lesion">lesions</a> in the mouth and pharynx. The virus can be transmitted throughout the course of the illness, but this happened most frequently during the first week of the rash when most of the skin lesions were intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> Infectivity waned in 7 to 10 days when scabs formed over the lesions, but the infected person was contagious until the last smallpox scab fell off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10367824_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10367824-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smallpox was highly contagious, but generally spread more slowly and less widely than some other viral diseases, perhaps because transmission required close contact and occurred after the onset of the rash. The overall rate of infection was also affected by the short duration of the infectious stage. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> areas, the number of smallpox infections was highest during the winter and spring. In tropical areas, seasonal variation was less evident and the disease was present throughout the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-10"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> Age distribution of smallpox infections depended on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acquired_immunity" title="Acquired immunity">acquired immunity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a> <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> declined over time and was probably lost within thirty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIP_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-AFIP-33">[33]</a></sup> Smallpox was not known to be transmitted by insects or animals and there was no <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic_carrier" title="Asymptomatic carrier">asymptomatic carrier</a> state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-11"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism">Mechanism</span></h2>
<p>Once inhaled, the variola virus invaded the mucus membranes of the mouth, throat, and respiratory tract. From there, it migrated to regional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymph_nodes" title="Lymph nodes">lymph nodes</a> and began to multiply. In the initial growth phase, the virus seemed to move from cell to cell, but by around the 12th day, widespread <a href="/wiki/Lysis" title="Lysis">lysis</a> of infected cells occurred and the virus could be found in the bloodstream in large numbers, a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Viremia" title="Viremia">viremia</a>. This resulted in the second wave of multiplication in the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>, and lymph nodes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<p>The clinical definition of ordinary smallpox is an illness with acute onset of fever equal to or greater than 38.3 °C (101 °F) followed by a rash characterized by firm, deep-seated vesicles or pustules in the same stage of development without other apparent cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-12"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> When a clinical case was observed, smallpox was confirmed using laboratory tests.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">Microscopically</a>, poxviruses produce characteristic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cytoplasmic" title="Cytoplasmic">cytoplasmic</a> <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_bodies" title="Inclusion bodies">inclusion bodies</a>, the most important of which are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guarnieri_bodies" title="Guarnieri bodies">Guarnieri bodies</a>, and are the sites of <a href="/wiki/Viral_replication" title="Viral replication">viral replication</a>. Guarnieri bodies are readily identified in skin biopsies stained with hematoxylin and eosin, and appear as pink blobs. They are found in virtually all poxvirus infections but the absence of Guarnieri bodies could not be used to rule out smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The diagnosis of an orthopoxvirus infection can also be made rapidly by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_microscopy" title="Electron microscopy">electron microscopic</a> examination of pustular fluid or scabs. All orthopoxviruses exhibit identical brick-shaped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virions" title="Virions">virions</a> by electron microscopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIP_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-AFIP-33">[33]</a></sup> If particles with the characteristic morphology of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpesvirus" title="Herpesvirus">herpesviruses</a> are seen this will eliminate smallpox and other orthopoxvirus infections.
</p><p>Definitive laboratory identification of <i>Variola virus</i> involved growing the virus on <a href="/wiki/Chorioallantoic_membrane" title="Chorioallantoic membrane">chorioallantoic membrane</a> (part of a chicken <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>) and examining the resulting pock lesions under defined temperature conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Strains were characterized by <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> (PCR) and <a href="/wiki/Restriction_fragment_length_polymorphism" title="Restriction fragment length polymorphism">restriction fragment length polymorphism</a> (RFLP) analysis. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serologic" title="Serologic">Serologic</a> tests and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzyme_linked_immunosorbent_assay" title="Enzyme linked immunosorbent assay">enzyme linked immunosorbent assays</a> (ELISA), which measured <i>Variola virus</i>-specific immunoglobulin and antigen were also developed to assist in the diagnosis of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">Chickenpox</a> was commonly confused with smallpox in the immediate post-eradication era. Chickenpox and smallpox could be distinguished by several methods. Unlike smallpox, chickenpox does not usually affect the palms and soles. Additionally, chickenpox pustules are of varying size due to variations in the timing of pustule eruption: smallpox pustules are all very nearly the same size since the viral effect progresses more uniformly. A variety of laboratory methods were available for detecting chickenpox in the evaluation of suspected smallpox cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-13"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Smallpox_vaccine" title="Smallpox vaccine">Smallpox vaccine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_vaccine_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1577" data-file-width="2647" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Smallpox_vaccine_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Smallpox_vaccine_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Smallpox_vaccine_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Smallpox_vaccine_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Smallpox_vaccine_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Smallpox_vaccine_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_vaccine_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Components of a modern smallpox <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> kit including the <a href="/wiki/Diluent" title="Diluent">diluent</a>, a vial of Dryvax vaccinia vaccine, and a <a href="/wiki/Bifurcated_needle" title="Bifurcated needle">bifurcated needle</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest procedure used to prevent smallpox was <a href="/wiki/Inoculation" title="Inoculation">inoculation</a> with <i>variola minor</i> (known as variolation after the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox_vaccine" title="Smallpox vaccine">smallpox vaccine</a> to avoid possible confusion), which likely occurred in India, Africa, and China well before the practice arrived in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rie2005_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rie2005-14">[14]</a></sup> The idea that inoculation originated in India has been challenged, as few of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> medical texts described the process of inoculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Accounts of inoculation against smallpox in China can be found as early as the late 10th century, and the procedure was widely practiced by the 16th century, during the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Temple_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Temple-69">[69]</a></sup> If successful, inoculation produced lasting <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> to smallpox. Because the person was infected with <i>Variola virus</i>, a severe infection could result, and the person could transmit smallpox to others. Variolation had a 0.5–2 percent mortality rate, considerably less than the 20–30 percent mortality rate of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-14"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> Two reports on the Chinese practice of <a href="/wiki/Inoculation" title="Inoculation">inoculation</a> were received by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> in London in 1700; one by Dr. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lister" title="Martin Lister">Martin Lister</a> who received a report by an employee of the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> stationed in China and another by <a href="/wiki/Clopton_Havers" title="Clopton Havers">Clopton Havers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lady_Mary_Wortley_Montagu" title="Lady Mary Wortley Montagu">Lady Mary Wortley Montagu</a> observed smallpox inoculation during her stay in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, writing detailed accounts of the practice in her letters, and enthusiastically promoted the procedure in England upon her return in 1718.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> (1742), the Turks derived their use of inoculation from neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Circassia" title="Circassia">Circassia</a>. Voltaire does not speculate on where the Circassians derived their technique from, though he reports that the Chinese have practiced it "these hundred years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voltaire_1742_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voltaire_1742-72">[72]</a></sup> In 1721, <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Mather#Advocacy_for_smallpox_inoculation" title="Cotton Mather">Cotton Mather</a> and colleagues provoked controversy in Boston by inoculating hundreds. After publishing <i>The present method of inoculating for the small-pox</i> in 1767, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Dimsdale#Careers" title="Thomas Dimsdale">Dr Thomas Dimsdale</a> was invited to Russia to variolate the Empress <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a> of Russia and her son, <a href="/wiki/Paul_I_of_Russia" title="Paul I of Russia">Grand Duke Paul</a>, which he successfully did in 1768.  In 1796, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a>, a doctor in <a href="/wiki/Berkeley,_Gloucestershire" title="Berkeley, Gloucestershire">Berkeley, Gloucestershire</a>, rural England, discovered that immunity to smallpox could be produced by inoculating a person with material from a cowpox lesion. Cowpox is a poxvirus in the same family as variola. Jenner called the material used for inoculation vaccine from the <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root word</a> <i>vacca</i>, which is Latin for cow. The procedure was much safer than variolation and did not involve a risk of smallpox transmission. Vaccination to prevent smallpox was soon practiced all over the world. During the 19th century, the cowpox virus used for smallpox vaccination was replaced by the vaccinia virus. Vaccinia is in the same family as cowpox and variola but is genetically distinct from both. The origin of the vaccinia virus and how it came to be in the vaccine are not known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-15"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_cow_pock.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="1279" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/The_cow_pock.jpg/310px-The_cow_pock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/The_cow_pock.jpg/465px-The_cow_pock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/The_cow_pock.jpg/620px-The_cow_pock.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_cow_pock.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1802 cartoon by <a href="/wiki/James_Gillray" title="James Gillray">James Gillray</a> of the early controversy surrounding <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a>'s vaccination procedure, showing using his cowpox-derived <a href="/wiki/Smallpox_vaccine" title="Smallpox vaccine">smallpox vaccine</a> causing cattle to emerge from patients.</div></div></div>
<p>The current formulation of the smallpox vaccine is a live virus preparation of the infectious vaccinia virus. The vaccine is given using a <a href="/wiki/Bifurcated_needle" title="Bifurcated needle">bifurcated (two-pronged) needle</a> that is dipped into the vaccine solution. The needle is used to prick the skin (usually the upper arm) several times in a few seconds. If successful, a red and itchy bump develops at the vaccine site in three or four days. In the first week, the bump becomes a large blister (called a "Jennerian vesicle") which fills with pus and begins to drain. During the second week, the blister begins to dry up, and a scab forms. The scab falls off in the third week, leaving a small scar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> induced by the vaccinia vaccine are cross-protective for other orthopoxviruses, such as monkeypox, cowpox, and variola (smallpox) viruses. Neutralizing antibodies are detectable 10 days after first-time vaccination and seven days after revaccination. Historically, the vaccine has been effective in preventing smallpox infection in 95 percent of those vaccinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCvac_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCvac-74">[74]</a></sup> Smallpox vaccination provides a high level of immunity for three to five years and decreasing immunity thereafter. If a person is vaccinated again later, the immunity lasts even longer. Studies of smallpox cases in Europe in the 1950s and 1960s demonstrated that the fatality rate among persons vaccinated less than 10 years before exposure was 1.3 percent; it was 7 percent among those vaccinated 11 to 20 years prior, and 11 percent among those vaccinated 20 or more years before infection. By contrast, 52 percent of unvaccinated persons died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg/220px-Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg/330px-Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg/440px-Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_vaccine_injection.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A demonstration by medical personnel on use of a bifurcated needle to deliver the smallpox vaccine, 2002.</div></div></div>
<p>There are side effects and risks associated with the smallpox vaccine. In the past, about 1 out of 1,000 people vaccinated for the first time experienced serious, but non-life-threatening, reactions, including toxic or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergic_reaction" title="Allergic reaction">allergic reaction</a> at the site of the vaccination (<a href="/wiki/Erythema_multiforme" title="Erythema multiforme">erythema multiforme</a>), spread of the vaccinia virus to other parts of the body, and spread to other individuals. Potentially life-threatening reactions occurred in 14 to 500 people out of every 1 million people vaccinated for the first time. Based on past experience, it is estimated that 1 or 2 people in 1 million (0.000198 percent) who receive the vaccine may die as a result, most often the result of postvaccinial <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">encephalitis</a> or severe <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a> in the area of vaccination (called <a href="/wiki/Progressive_vaccinia" title="Progressive vaccinia">progressive vaccinia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCvac_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDCvac-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Given these risks, as smallpox became effectively eradicated and the number of naturally occurring cases fell below the number of vaccine-induced illnesses and deaths, routine childhood vaccination was discontinued in the United States in 1972 and was abandoned in most European countries in the early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Routine vaccination of health care workers was discontinued in the U.S. in 1976, and among military recruits in 1990 (although military personnel deploying to the Middle East and Korea still receive the vaccination<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>). By 1986, routine vaccination had ceased in all countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007Fact_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007Fact-10">[10]</a></sup> It is now primarily recommended for laboratory workers at risk for occupational exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook_32-16"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook-32">[32]</a></sup> However, the possibility of smallpox virus being used as a biological weapon has rekindled interest in the development of newer vaccines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzger_2007_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger_2007-78">[78]</a></sup> The smallpox vaccine is also effective in, and therefore administered for, the prevention of <a href="/wiki/Monkeypox" title="Monkeypox">monkeypox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid36179973_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid36179973-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h2>
<p>Smallpox vaccination within three days of exposure will prevent or significantly lessen the severity of smallpox symptoms in the vast majority of people. Vaccination four to seven days after exposure can offer some protection from disease or may modify the severity of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCvac_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDCvac-74">[74]</a></sup> Other than vaccination, treatment of smallpox is primarily supportive, such as wound care and infection control, fluid therapy, and possible <a href="/wiki/Ventilator" title="Ventilator">ventilator</a> assistance. Flat and hemorrhagic types of smallpox are treated with the same therapies used to treat <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Fluid_replacement" title="Fluid replacement">fluid resuscitation</a>. People with semi-confluent and confluent types of smallpox may have therapeutic issues similar to patients with extensive skin <a href="/wiki/Burn" title="Burn">burns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenner4_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fenner4-80">[80]</a></sup>
